          reduce jargon, and maintain a scholarly tone. The revision should
          adhere to Markdown formatting and follow a Context-Content-Conclusion
          (C-C-C) structure. This structure begins by setting the stage with one
          or two sentences (Context), progresses through what is known from the
          literature (Content), and concludes by highlighting an aspect of the
          knowledge gap the manuscript addresses (Conclusion). Your revision
          should: 1) preserve the original information as much as possible, with
          minimal changes to the text, 2) ensure most references to scientific
          articles are kept exactly as they appear in the original text; these
          references are crucial for academic integrity and may appear with the
          format "[@TYPE:ID]" such as "[@pmid:33931583;
          @doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225; @pmid:31036433]", and 4) the revised
          paragraph must encapsulate the entire revision, following the C-C-C
          structure, within a single, cohesive paragraph.


          Input paragraph: Genes work together in context-specific networks to
          karry out different functions [@pmid:19104045; @doi:10.1038/ng.3259].

          mechanisms that, when disrupted, commonly result in tissue- and cell
          lineage-specific pathologies [@doi:10.1038/s41588-018-0081-4;
          @doi:10.1016/j.ajhg.2018.04.002; @doi:10.1038/ncomm96890].
          Nevertheless, further research is needed to fully understand the
          intricate interplay between genes and their regulatory elements in

          understanding of complex diseases and lead to more effective
          treatments.

          individual genes, overlooking complex interactions at the network
          level. This limitation presents a significant gap in our understanding
          of how genetic associations contribute to disease etiology and drug
          mechanisms.

          jargon, and maintain a scholarly tone. The revision should adhere to
          Markdown formatting and follow a Context-Content-Conclusion (C-C-C)
          structure. This structure begins by setting the stage with one or two
          sentences (Context), progresses through what is known from the
          literature (Content), and concludes by highlighting an aspect of the
          knowledge gap the manuscript addresses (Conclusion). Your revision
          should: 1) preserve the original information as much as possible, with
          minimal changes to the text, 2) ensure most references to scientific
          articles are kept exactly as they appear in the original text; these
          references are crucial for academic integrity and may appear with the
          format "[@TYPE:ID]" such as "[@pmid:33931583;
          @doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225; @pmid:31036433]", and 4) the revised
          paragraph must encapsulate the entire revision, following the C-C-C
          structure, within a single, cohesive paragraph.


          Input paragraph: TWAS relies on testing whether perturbations in gene
          regulatory mechanisms mediate the association between genetic variants
          and human diseases [@doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1009482;
          @doi:10.1038/ng.3506; @doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1007889;
          @doi:10.1038/ng.3367], and these approaches have been highly
          successful not only in understanding disease etiology at the
          transcriptome level [@pmid:33931583; @doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225;
          @pmid:31036433] but also in disease-risk prediction (polygenic scores)
          [@doi:10.1186/s13059-021-02591-w] and drug repurposing
          [@doi:10.1038/nn.4618] tasks. Given the availability of gene
          expression data across several tissues [@doi:10.1038/nbt.3838;
          @doi:10.1038/s41467-018-03751-6; @doi:10.1126/science.aaz1776;
          @doi:10.1186/s13040-020-00216-9], an effective approach to identify
          these biological processes is the transcription-wide association study
          (TWAS), which integrates expression quantitative trait loci (eQTLs)
          data to provide a mechanistic interpretation for GWAS findings.
          However, TWAS works at the individual gene level, which does not
          capture more complex interactions at the network level.

          Transcriptome-wide association studies (TWAS) have emerged as a
          powerful tool in understanding the genetic underpinnings of human
          diseases [@doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1009482; @doi:10.1038/ng.3506;
          @doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1007889; @doi:10.1038/ng.3367]. These
          studies operate by examining whether alterations in gene regulatory
          mechanisms mediate the relationship between genetic variants and
          diseases [@pmid:33931583; @doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225;
          @pmid:31036433]. The success of TWAS extends beyond mere disease
          etiology comprehension at the transcriptome level, as it also aids in
          predicting disease risk through polygenic scores
          [@doi:10.1186/s13059-021-02591-w] and facilitates drug repurposing
          efforts [@doi:10.1038/nn.4618].


          Given the abundance of gene expression data across various tissues
          [@doi:10.1038/nbt.3838; @doi:10.1038/s41467-018-03751-6;
          @doi:10.1126/science.aaz1776; @doi:10.1186/s13040-020-00216-9], TWAS
          offers an effective means to identify these biological processes by
          integrating expression quantitative trait loci (eQTLs) data, thereby
          providing a mechanistic interpretation for genome-wide association
          study (GWAS) findings. However, a significant limitation of TWAS is
          that it operates at the individual gene level, which inherently fails
          to capture more complex interactions occurring at the network level.
          This manuscript aims to address this knowledge gap by proposing an
          innovative approach to extend TWAS beyond single genes and into the
          realm of gene networks.

          capture more complex interactions at the network level. This knowledge
          gap forms the basis of our study, where we aim to extend TWAS analysis
          to incorporate gene co-expression networks and cluster complex traits
          for a better understanding of disease etiology and drug mechanisms.

          mechanistic interpretation of genome-wide association studies (GWAS)
          findings. However, the current limitation of TWAS is its focus on

          @doi:10.1186/s13040-020-00216-9], an effective approach to identify

          tissues [[@doi:10.1038/nbt.3838; @doi:10.1038/s41467-018-03751-6;
          @doi:10.1126/science.aaz1776; @doi:10.1186/s13040-020-00216-9]], a
          powerful method for uncovering biological processes is the

          expression quantitative trait loci (eQTL) data to offer an explanation
          behind genome-wide association studies (GWAS) findings.

          capture intricate interactions at the network level. It functions by
          examining whether alterations in gene regulatory mechanisms mediate

          @doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1007889; @doi:10.1038/ng.3367]]. These

          risk using polygenic scores [[@doi:10.1186/s13059-021-02591-w]], and
          repurposing drugs for new therapeutic uses [[@doi:10.1038/nn.4618]].


          Conclusion: Despite these successes, TWAS's focus on individual genes
          leaves a gap in understanding complex network interactions, which is
          crucial for fully comprehending disease etiology and drug mechanisms.
          This manuscript aims to address this knowledge gap by projecting
          genetic associations through gene expression patterns, thereby
          highlighting disease etiology and drug mechanisms.

              prediction (polygenic scores) [@doi:10.1186/s13059-021-02591-w]
              and drug repurposing tasks [@doi:10.1038/nn.4618]. However, TWAS
              operates at the individual gene level, which may not capture more
